'To determine which side of the pentagon will touch the point \\(x = 2009\\) on the \\(x\\)-axis, we need to calculate the total distance the pentagon rolls along the \\(x\\)-axis and find the position of each side as it touches the axis.\n\nThe pentagon has sides \\(AB = 3\\), \\(BC = 4\\), \\(CD = 6\\), \\(DE = 3\\), and \\(EA = 7\\). The perimeter of the pentagon is:\n\n\\[\nAB + BC + CD + DE + EA = 3 + 4 + 6 + 3 + 7 = 23.\n\\]\n\nThe pentagon rolls along the \\(x\\)-axis, and each time it completes a full roll, it covers a distance equal to its perimeter, 23 units. We need to find the remainder when 2009 is divided by 23 to determine how far along the perimeter the pentagon is at \\(x = 2009\\).\n\nCalculating the remainder:\n\n\\[\n2009 \\div 23 = 87 \\text{ remainder } 8.\n\\]\n\nThis means that after 87 complete rolls, the pentagon has rolled an additional 8 units along the \\(x\\)-axis. We now determine which side of the pentagon is touching the \\(x\\)-axis after rolling 8 units.\n\nStarting from \\(A\\) at the origin, the sequence of sides touching the \\(x\\)-axis is as follows:\n\n1. \\(AB\\) covers 3 units.\n2. \\(BC\\) covers the next 4 units (total 7 units).\n3. \\(CD\\) starts at 7 units and covers the next 6 units (total 13 units).\n\nSince 8 is between 7 and 13, the side \\(CD\\) is touching the \\(x\\)-axis at \\(x = 2009\\).\n\nTherefore, the side that touches the point \\(x = 2009\\) on the \\(x\\)-axis is \\(\\boxed{CD}\\).'
"To find the maximum value of the function \\( f(x) = x(1-x)^5 \\) for \\( 0 \\le x \\le 1 \\), we start by taking the derivative of \\( f(x) \\) with respect to \\( x \\).\n\nFirst, apply the product rule to differentiate \\( f(x) = x(1-x)^5 \\):\n\\[\nf'(x) = \\frac{d}{dx}[x] \\cdot (1-x)^5 + x \\cdot \\frac{d}{dx}[(1-x)^5].\n\\]\n\nThe derivative of \\( x \\) is 1, and using the chain rule, the derivative of \\( (1-x)^5 \\) is:\n\\[\n\\frac{d}{dx}[(1-x)^5] = 5(1-x)^4 \\cdot (-1) = -5(1-x)^4.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the product rule gives:\n\\[\nf'(x) = (1)(1-x)^5 + x(-5)(1-x)^4 = (1-x)^5 - 5x(1-x)^4.\n\\]\n\nFactor out \\( (1-x)^4 \\) from the expression:\n\\[\nf'(x) = (1-x)^4[(1-x) - 5x] = (1-x)^4(1 - 6x).\n\\]\n\nTo find the critical points, set \\( f'(x) = 0 \\):\n\\[\n(1-x)^4(1 - 6x) = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis gives two cases:\n1. \\( (1-x)^4 = 0 \\) which implies \\( x = 1 \\).\n2. \\( 1 - 6x = 0 \\) which implies \\( x = \\frac{1}{6} \\).\n\nWe need to evaluate \\( f(x) \\) at the critical points and the endpoints of the interval \\( [0, 1] \\).\n\n1. At \\( x = 0 \\):\n   \\[\n   f(0) = 0 \\cdot (1-0)^5 = 0.\n   \\]\n\n2. At \\( x = \\frac{1}{6} \\):\n   \\[\n   f\\left(\\frac{1}{6}\\right) = \\frac{1}{6} \\left(1 - \\frac{1}{6}\\right)^5 = \\frac{1}{6} \\left(\\frac{5}{6}\\right)^5 = \\frac{1}{6} \\cdot \\frac{3125}{7776} = \\frac{3125}{46656}.\n   \\]\n\n3. At \\( x = 1 \\):\n   \\[\n   f(1) = 1 \\cdot (1-1)^5 = 0.\n   \\]\n\nComparing these values, the maximum value of \\( f(x) \\) on the interval \\( [0, 1] \\) is \\( \\frac{3125}{46656} \\).\n\nThus, the maximum value of \\( x(1-x)^5 \\) for \\( 0 \\le x \\le 1 \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3125}{46656}}\\)."
'To solve the inequality \\(\\frac{1}{r} > \\frac{1}{r-1} + \\frac{1}{r-4}\\), we first find a common denominator for the right-hand side:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{r-1} + \\frac{1}{r-4} = \\frac{r-4 + r-1}{(r-1)(r-4)} = \\frac{2r-5}{(r-1)(r-4)}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the inequality becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{r} > \\frac{2r-5}{(r-1)(r-4)}.\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n(r-1)(r-4) > r(2r-5).\n\\]\n\nExpanding both sides, we have:\n\n\\[\nr^2 - 5r + 4 > 2r^2 - 5r.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\nr^2 - 5r + 4 > 2r^2 - 5r.\n\\]\n\nSubtract \\(r^2 - 5r\\) from both sides:\n\n\\[\n4 > r^2.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\nr^2 < 4.\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\n-2 < r < 2.\n\\]\n\nHowever, we must also consider the restrictions imposed by the original inequality, specifically the denominators. The values \\(r = 0\\), \\(r = 1\\), and \\(r = 4\\) make the denominators zero, so they must be excluded from the solution set.\n\nThus, the solution in interval notation, excluding these points, is:\n\n\\[\n(-2, 0) \\cup (0, 1) \\cup (1, 2).\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the real values of \\(r\\) that satisfy the inequality are \\((-2, 0) \\cup (0, 1) \\cup (1, 2)\\).'
'To solve the problem, we start by considering the given equation:\n\n\\[\n\\left( x + \\frac{1}{y} \\right) \\left( y + \\frac{1}{z} \\right) \\left( z + \\frac{1}{x} \\right) = 8.\n\\]\n\nWe will use the AM-GM inequality to analyze the expression. By AM-GM, we have:\n\n\\[\nx + \\frac{1}{y} \\geq 2\\sqrt{\\frac{x}{y}},\n\\]\n\\[\ny + \\frac{1}{z} \\geq 2\\sqrt{\\frac{y}{z}},\n\\]\n\\[\nz + \\frac{1}{x} \\geq 2\\sqrt{\\frac{z}{x}}.\n\\]\n\nMultiplying these inequalities together, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\left( x + \\frac{1}{y} \\right) \\left( y + \\frac{1}{z} \\right) \\left( z + \\frac{1}{x} \\right) \\geq 8 \\sqrt{\\frac{x}{y} \\cdot \\frac{y}{z} \\cdot \\frac{z}{x}} = 8.\n\\]\n\nSince the product is equal to 8, equality must hold in each of the AM-GM inequalities. Equality in AM-GM occurs when the terms are equal, so we have:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{1}{y}, \\quad y = \\frac{1}{z}, \\quad z = \\frac{1}{x}.\n\\]\n\nFrom these equations, we can express each variable in terms of one of them. For instance, from \\(x = \\frac{1}{y}\\), we have \\(y = \\frac{1}{x}\\). Similarly, from \\(y = \\frac{1}{z}\\), we have \\(z = \\frac{1}{y} = x\\). Finally, from \\(z = \\frac{1}{x}\\), we have \\(x = \\frac{1}{z} = y\\).\n\nThus, we find that \\(x = y = z\\). Substituting back into the original equation, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\left( x + \\frac{1}{x} \\right)^3 = 8.\n\\]\n\nLet \\(a = x + \\frac{1}{x}\\). Then \\(a^3 = 8\\), which implies \\(a = 2\\) (since \\(a\\) must be positive). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\nx + \\frac{1}{x} = 2.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(x\\), we find:\n\n\\[\nx^2 - 2x + 1 = 0 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad (x-1)^2 = 0 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad x = 1.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(x = y = z = 1\\).\n\nTherefore, there is exactly one ordered triple \\((x, y, z)\\) of positive real numbers that satisfies the given equation, which is \\((1, 1, 1)\\).\n\nThe number of such ordered triples is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
'To find the quotient when \\(x^6 - 3\\) is divided by \\(x + 1\\), we can use polynomial long division.\n\n1. **Setup the division**: Divide \\(x^6 - 3\\) by \\(x + 1\\).\n\n2. **Divide the leading terms**: Divide the leading term of the dividend, \\(x^6\\), by the leading term of the divisor, \\(x\\), to get \\(x^5\\).\n\n3. **Multiply and subtract**: Multiply \\(x^5\\) by \\(x + 1\\) to get \\(x^6 + x^5\\). Subtract this from the original polynomial:\n   \\[\n   (x^6 - 3) - (x^6 + x^5) = -x^5 - 3.\n   \\]\n\n4. **Repeat the process**: Divide \\(-x^5\\) by \\(x\\) to get \\(-x^4\\). Multiply \\(-x^4\\) by \\(x + 1\\) to get \\(-x^5 - x^4\\). Subtract:\n   \\[\n   (-x^5 - 3) - (-x^5 - x^4) = x^4 - 3.\n   \\]\n\n5. **Continue dividing**: Divide \\(x^4\\) by \\(x\\) to get \\(x^3\\). Multiply \\(x^3\\) by \\(x + 1\\) to get \\(x^4 + x^3\\). Subtract:\n   \\[\n   (x^4 - 3) - (x^4 + x^3) = -x^3 - 3.\n   \\]\n\n6. **Continue the process**: Divide \\(-x^3\\) by \\(x\\) to get \\(-x^2\\). Multiply \\(-x^2\\) by \\(x + 1\\) to get \\(-x^3 - x^2\\). Subtract:\n   \\[\n   (-x^3 - 3) - (-x^3 - x^2) = x^2 - 3.\n   \\]\n\n7. **Continue dividing**: Divide \\(x^2\\) by \\(x\\) to get \\(x\\). Multiply \\(x\\) by \\(x + 1\\) to get \\(x^2 + x\\). Subtract:\n   \\[\n   (x^2 - 3) - (x^2 + x) = -x - 3.\n   \\]\n\n8. **Continue the process**: Divide \\(-x\\) by \\(x\\) to get \\(-1\\). Multiply \\(-1\\) by \\(x + 1\\) to get \\(-x - 1\\). Subtract:\n   \\[\n   (-x - 3) - (-x - 1) = -2.\n   \\]\n\nThe remainder is \\(-2\\), and the quotient is \\(x^5 - x^4 + x^3 - x^2 + x - 1\\).\n\nThus, the quotient when \\(x^6 - 3\\) is divided by \\(x + 1\\) is \\(\\boxed{x^5 - x^4 + x^3 - x^2 + x - 1}\\).'
