"The polynomial given is \n\n\\[\nx^{2016} + x^{2015} + \\dots + x + 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as \n\n\\[\n\\frac{x^{2017} - 1}{x - 1} = 0.\n\\]\n\nThe roots of this equation are the 2017th roots of unity, excluding \\(x = 1\\). Therefore, the roots are \n\n\\[\nx_k = e^{2\\pi i k / 2017} \\quad \\text{for } k = 1, 2, \\ldots, 2016.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \n\n\\[\n\\sum_{k=1}^{2016} \\frac{1}{(1 - x_k)^2}.\n\\]\n\nTo do this, we use the identity for the sum of reciprocals of squares of differences from 1 for roots of unity. For a polynomial \\(P(x)\\) with roots \\(x_1, x_2, \\ldots, x_n\\), the identity is:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{k=1}^{n} \\frac{1}{(1 - x_k)^2} = \\sum_{k=1}^{n} \\frac{P''(x_k)}{P'(x_k)^2}.\n\\]\n\nFor our polynomial \\(P(x) = \\frac{x^{2017} - 1}{x - 1}\\), the derivative is:\n\n\\[\nP'(x) = \\frac{(x^{2017} - 1)'(x - 1) - (x^{2017} - 1)(1)}{(x - 1)^2} = \\frac{2017x^{2016}(x - 1) - (x^{2017} - 1)}{(x - 1)^2}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\nP'(x) = \\frac{2017x^{2016}x - 2017x^{2016} - x^{2017} + 1}{(x - 1)^2} = \\frac{2016x^{2017} - 2017x^{2016} + 1}{(x - 1)^2}.\n\\]\n\nThe second derivative is:\n\n\\[\nP''(x) = \\left(\\frac{2016x^{2017} - 2017x^{2016} + 1}{(x - 1)^2}\\right)'.\n\\]\n\nHowever, we can use a known result for the sum of reciprocals of squares of differences from 1 for roots of unity. For the polynomial \\(x^{n} + x^{n-1} + \\cdots + x + 1\\), the sum is:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{k=1}^{n} \\frac{1}{(1 - x_k)^2} = \\frac{n^2}{4}.\n\\]\n\nIn our case, \\(n = 2016\\), so:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{k=1}^{2016} \\frac{1}{(1 - x_k)^2} = \\frac{2016^2}{4} = \\frac{4064256}{4} = 1016064.\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{1016064}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the distance between the two vertices of a hyperbola that has its foci at the midpoints of \\(\\overline{AB}\\) and \\(\\overline{CD}\\) of a unit square \\(ABCD\\), and passes through all the vertices of the square.\n\nFirst, let's place the unit square \\(ABCD\\) in the coordinate plane. We can set:\n- \\(A = (0, 0)\\),\n- \\(B = (1, 0)\\),\n- \\(C = (1, 1)\\),\n- \\(D = (0, 1)\\).\n\nThe midpoints of \\(\\overline{AB}\\) and \\(\\overline{CD}\\) are:\n- Midpoint of \\(\\overline{AB}\\): \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{2}, 0\\right)\\),\n- Midpoint of \\(\\overline{CD}\\): \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{2}, 1\\right)\\).\n\nThese points are the foci of the hyperbola. The hyperbola passes through all the vertices of the square, so it passes through points \\(A\\), \\(B\\), \\(C\\), and \\(D\\).\n\nThe general equation of a hyperbola with a vertical transverse axis centered at \\(\\left(\\frac{1}{2}, \\frac{1}{2}\\right)\\) is:\n\\[\n\\frac{(y - \\frac{1}{2})^2}{a^2} - \\frac{(x - \\frac{1}{2})^2}{b^2} = 1\n\\]\n\nThe distance between the foci is 1, which is \\(2c\\), so \\(c = \\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nThe relationship between \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\) for a hyperbola is:\n\\[\nc^2 = a^2 + b^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(c = \\frac{1}{2}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2 = a^2 + b^2 \\implies \\frac{1}{4} = a^2 + b^2\n\\]\n\nThe hyperbola passes through the vertices of the square. Let's use point \\(A = (0, 0)\\) to find a relationship between \\(a\\) and \\(b\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{\\left(0 - \\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2}{a^2} - \\frac{\\left(0 - \\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2}{b^2} = 1\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{\\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2}{a^2} - \\frac{\\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2}{b^2} = 1\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{\\frac{1}{4}}{a^2} - \\frac{\\frac{1}{4}}{b^2} = 1\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{a^2} - \\frac{1}{b^2} = 4\n\\]\n\nNow we have two equations:\n1. \\(\\frac{1}{4} = a^2 + b^2\\)\n2. \\(\\frac{1}{a^2} - \\frac{1}{b^2} = 4\\)\n\nFrom the second equation, we can express \\(\\frac{1}{b^2}\\) in terms of \\(\\frac{1}{a^2}\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{b^2} = \\frac{1}{a^2} - 4\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\frac{1}{b^2}\\) into the first equation:\n\\[\na^2 + \\frac{1}{\\left(\\frac{1}{a^2} - 4\\right)} = \\frac{1}{4}\n\\]\n\nSolving these equations simultaneously, we find:\n- \\(a^2 = \\frac{1}{8}\\)\n- \\(b^2 = \\frac{1}{8}\\)\n\nThe vertices of the hyperbola are at a distance \\(2a\\) from the center along the transverse axis. Since the transverse axis is vertical, the distance between the vertices is:\n\\[\n2a = 2 \\times \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{8}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the distance between the two vertices of the hyperbola is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{2}}\\)."
"To find a rational function \\( g(x) \\) that generates the sequence \\(\\frac{1}{2}, \\ \\frac{2}{3}, \\ \\frac{3}{4}, \\ \\frac{4}{5}, \\ \\dots\\), we need \\( g(x) \\) such that plugging any term of the sequence into \\( g(x) \\) gives the next term.\n\nLet's denote the \\( n \\)-th term of the sequence by \\( a_n = \\frac{n}{n+1} \\). We want \\( g(a_n) = a_{n+1} = \\frac{n+1}{n+2} \\).\n\nTo find \\( g(x) \\), consider the transformation from \\( a_n = \\frac{n}{n+1} \\) to \\( a_{n+1} = \\frac{n+1}{n+2} \\). We can express this transformation as:\n\n\\[\ng\\left(\\frac{n}{n+1}\\right) = \\frac{n+1}{n+2}\n\\]\n\nNotice that:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{n+1}{n+2} = \\frac{\\frac{n}{n+1} + \\frac{1}{n+1}}{\\frac{n}{n+1} + \\frac{2}{n+1}}\n\\]\n\nThis suggests that the function \\( g(x) \\) can be expressed as:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x + \\frac{1}{n+1}}{x + \\frac{2}{n+1}}\n\\]\n\nHowever, since \\( x = \\frac{n}{n+1} \\), we have:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x + \\frac{1}{1-x}}{x + \\frac{2}{1-x}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying this expression, we find:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x(1-x) + 1}{x(1-x) + 2}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( x = \\frac{n}{n+1} \\) into this expression, we verify that:\n\n\\[\ng\\left(\\frac{n}{n+1}\\right) = \\frac{\\frac{n}{n+1} + \\frac{1}{n+1}}{\\frac{n}{n+1} + \\frac{2}{n+1}} = \\frac{n+1}{n+2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the rational function \\( g(x) \\) that generates the sequence is:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x + \\frac{1}{1-x}}{x + \\frac{2}{1-x}}\n\\]\n\nHowever, a simpler form of this function is:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x}{x + \\frac{1}{1-x}}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x}{x + 1}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the rational function \\( g(x) \\) that generates the sequence is:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x}{x + 1}\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is:\n\n\\[\ng(x) = \\frac{x}{x + 1}\n\\]"
'To solve the equation \\(x(x + 1)(x + 2)(x + 3) + 1 = 379^2\\), we start by rewriting the left-hand side:\n\n\\[\nx(x + 1)(x + 2)(x + 3) + 1 = (x^2 + 3x)(x^2 + 3x + 2) + 1.\n\\]\n\nLet \\(y = x^2 + 3x\\). Then the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\ny(y + 2) + 1 = y^2 + 2y + 1 = (y + 1)^2.\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n(y + 1)^2 = 379^2.\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we have:\n\n\\[\ny + 1 = 379 \\quad \\text{or} \\quad y + 1 = -379.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(y = x^2 + 3x\\) is non-negative for positive integer \\(x\\), we discard the negative solution. Therefore:\n\n\\[\ny + 1 = 379 \\implies y = 378.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back for \\(y\\), we have:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + 3x = 378.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + 3x - 378 = 0.\n\\]\n\nWe solve this using the quadratic formula \\(x = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\\), where \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = 3\\), and \\(c = -378\\):\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-3 \\pm \\sqrt{3^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot (-378)}}{2 \\cdot 1} = \\frac{-3 \\pm \\sqrt{9 + 1512}}{2} = \\frac{-3 \\pm \\sqrt{1521}}{2}.\n\\]\n\nCalculating the square root:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{1521} = 39.\n\\]\n\nThus, the solutions for \\(x\\) are:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-3 + 39}{2} = \\frac{36}{2} = 18 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad x = \\frac{-3 - 39}{2} = \\frac{-42}{2} = -21.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(x\\) is a positive integer, we have:\n\n\\[\nx = 18.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(x\\) is \\(\\boxed{18}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the sum of the six pairwise distances between the four distinct complex solutions \\( z_1, z_2, z_3, \\) and \\( z_4 \\) of the given equation:\n\n\\[\nz^4 - 6z^2 + 8z + 1 = -4(z^3 - z + 2)i.\n\\]\n\nFirst, let's rewrite the equation in a more standard form:\n\n\\[\nz^4 - 6z^2 + 8z + 1 + 4(z^3 - z + 2)i = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis equation is a polynomial of degree 4 in \\( z \\), and it has complex coefficients. The roots of this polynomial are \\( z_1, z_2, z_3, \\) and \\( z_4 \\).\n\nThe sum of the pairwise distances between these roots is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{1 \\le i < j \\le 4} |z_i - z_j|.\n\\]\n\nTo find this sum, we can use a known result from complex analysis and algebraic geometry: for a polynomial \\( P(z) \\) of degree \\( n \\) with roots \\( z_1, z_2, \\ldots, z_n \\), the sum of the squares of the pairwise distances between the roots is related to the coefficients of the polynomial. Specifically, if \\( P(z) = a_n z^n + a_{n-1} z^{n-1} + \\cdots + a_1 z + a_0 \\), then:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{1 \\le i < j \\le n} |z_i - z_j|^2 = n \\left( \\sum_{k=1}^{n} |z_k|^2 \\right) - \\left| \\sum_{k=1}^{n} z_k \\right|^2.\n\\]\n\nFor our polynomial, we have \\( n = 4 \\). The polynomial can be written as:\n\n\\[\nP(z) = z^4 + 4iz^3 - 6z^2 - 4iz + 9.\n\\]\n\nThe sum of the roots \\( z_1 + z_2 + z_3 + z_4 \\) is given by Vieta's formulas as the negative of the coefficient of \\( z^3 \\) divided by the leading coefficient, which is:\n\n\\[\nz_1 + z_2 + z_3 + z_4 = -4i.\n\\]\n\nThe sum of the squares of the roots \\( z_1^2 + z_2^2 + z_3^2 + z_4^2 \\) can be found using the identity:\n\n\\[\n(z_1 + z_2 + z_3 + z_4)^2 = z_1^2 + z_2^2 + z_3^2 + z_4^2 + 2\\sum_{1 \\le i < j \\le 4} z_i z_j.\n\\]\n\nThe sum of the products of the roots taken two at a time \\( \\sum_{1 \\le i < j \\le 4} z_i z_j \\) is given by Vieta's formulas as the coefficient of \\( z^2 \\) divided by the leading coefficient:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{1 \\le i < j \\le 4} z_i z_j = -6.\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n(-4i)^2 = z_1^2 + z_2^2 + z_3^2 + z_4^2 + 2(-6).\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-16 = z_1^2 + z_2^2 + z_3^2 + z_4^2 - 12.\n\\]\n\n\\[\nz_1^2 + z_2^2 + z_3^2 + z_4^2 = -4.\n\\]\n\nNow, using the formula for the sum of the squares of the pairwise distances:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{1 \\le i < j \\le 4} |z_i - z_j|^2 = 4(-4) - |-4i|^2 = -16 - 16 = -32.\n\\]\n\nThe sum of the pairwise distances is the square root of the sum of the squares of the pairwise distances:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{1 \\le i < j \\le 4} |z_i - z_j| = \\sqrt{-32}.\n\\]\n\nHowever, this result seems incorrect because the sum of distances should be a real number. Let's reconsider the approach. The sum of the pairwise distances is not directly given by the sum of the squares of the distances. Instead, we need to find the actual distances.\n\nGiven the complexity of the problem and the potential for algebraic manipulation errors, a more straightforward approach is to use symmetry or numerical methods to approximate the roots and calculate the distances directly. However, without additional information or computational tools, we cannot proceed further analytically.\n\nTherefore, the problem requires either a numerical solution or additional insights into the symmetry or specific properties of the polynomial roots."
'Since \\( f(x) \\) is a polynomial with rational coefficients, the conjugates of any irrational roots must also be roots of the polynomial. Therefore, the roots of \\( f(x) \\) are \\( 1 + 2\\sqrt{3} \\), \\( 1 - 2\\sqrt{3} \\), \\( 3 - \\sqrt{2} \\), and \\( 3 + \\sqrt{2} \\).\n\nThe polynomial \\( f(x) \\) can be expressed as:\n\\[\nf(x) = a(x - (1 + 2\\sqrt{3}))(x - (1 - 2\\sqrt{3}))(x - (3 - \\sqrt{2}))(x - (3 + \\sqrt{2}))\n\\]\n\nFirst, simplify the factors:\n\\[\n(x - (1 + 2\\sqrt{3}))(x - (1 - 2\\sqrt{3})) = ((x - 1) - 2\\sqrt{3})((x - 1) + 2\\sqrt{3}) = (x - 1)^2 - (2\\sqrt{3})^2 = (x - 1)^2 - 12\n\\]\n\\[\n= x^2 - 2x + 1 - 12 = x^2 - 2x - 11\n\\]\n\nSimilarly,\n\\[\n(x - (3 - \\sqrt{2}))(x - (3 + \\sqrt{2})) = ((x - 3) - \\sqrt{2})((x - 3) + \\sqrt{2}) = (x - 3)^2 - (\\sqrt{2})^2 = (x - 3)^2 - 2\n\\]\n\\[\n= x^2 - 6x + 9 - 2 = x^2 - 6x + 7\n\\]\n\nThus, the polynomial becomes:\n\\[\nf(x) = a(x^2 - 2x - 11)(x^2 - 6x + 7)\n\\]\n\nNext, expand the product:\n\\[\n(x^2 - 2x - 11)(x^2 - 6x + 7) = x^4 - 6x^3 + 7x^2 - 2x^3 + 12x^2 - 14x - 11x^2 + 66x - 77\n\\]\n\\[\n= x^4 - 8x^3 + 8x^2 + 52x - 77\n\\]\n\nSince \\( f(0) = -154 \\), we have:\n\\[\nf(0) = a(-77) = -154 \\implies a = \\frac{-154}{-77} = 2\n\\]\n\nThus, the polynomial is:\n\\[\nf(x) = 2(x^4 - 8x^3 + 8x^2 + 52x - 77)\n\\]\n\nNow, calculate \\( f(1) \\):\n\\[\nf(1) = 2(1^4 - 8 \\cdot 1^3 + 8 \\cdot 1^2 + 52 \\cdot 1 - 77)\n\\]\n\\[\n= 2(1 - 8 + 8 + 52 - 77)\n\\]\n\\[\n= 2(-24)\n\\]\n\\[\n= -48\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\( f(1) = \\boxed{-48} \\).'
"To find the minimum value of \\(x^2 + y^2\\) given the condition \\(x^2 + y^2 = 4xy\\), we start by rewriting the condition:\n\n\\[ x^2 + y^2 - 4xy = 0. \\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[ (x - y)^2 = 2xy. \\]\n\nWe want to minimize \\(x^2 + y^2\\). Notice that:\n\n\\[ x^2 + y^2 = (x-y)^2 + 2xy. \\]\n\nSubstituting the condition \\((x-y)^2 = 2xy\\) into this expression, we have:\n\n\\[ x^2 + y^2 = 2xy + 2xy = 4xy. \\]\n\nThus, the expression \\(x^2 + y^2\\) is equal to \\(4xy\\) under the given condition. To minimize \\(x^2 + y^2\\), we need to minimize \\(4xy\\).\n\nLet's express \\(x\\) and \\(y\\) in terms of a single variable. Assume \\(x = ky\\) for some constant \\(k\\). Then:\n\n\\[ x^2 + y^2 = k^2y^2 + y^2 = (k^2 + 1)y^2, \\]\n\\[ 4xy = 4ky^2. \\]\n\nEquating these two expressions gives:\n\n\\[ (k^2 + 1)y^2 = 4ky^2. \\]\n\nDividing both sides by \\(y^2\\) (assuming \\(y \\neq 0\\)):\n\n\\[ k^2 + 1 = 4k. \\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[ k^2 - 4k + 1 = 0. \\]\n\nSolving this quadratic equation for \\(k\\) using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[ k = \\frac{4 \\pm \\sqrt{16 - 4}}{2} = \\frac{4 \\pm \\sqrt{12}}{2} = \\frac{4 \\pm 2\\sqrt{3}}{2} = 2 \\pm \\sqrt{3}. \\]\n\nThus, \\(k = 2 + \\sqrt{3}\\) or \\(k = 2 - \\sqrt{3}\\).\n\nFor each \\(k\\), we calculate \\(x^2 + y^2\\):\n\n1. If \\(k = 2 + \\sqrt{3}\\), then:\n\n   \\[ x^2 + y^2 = (k^2 + 1)y^2 = ((2 + \\sqrt{3})^2 + 1)y^2. \\]\n\n   \\[ (2 + \\sqrt{3})^2 = 4 + 4\\sqrt{3} + 3 = 7 + 4\\sqrt{3}. \\]\n\n   \\[ x^2 + y^2 = (8 + 4\\sqrt{3})y^2. \\]\n\n2. If \\(k = 2 - \\sqrt{3}\\), then:\n\n   \\[ x^2 + y^2 = (k^2 + 1)y^2 = ((2 - \\sqrt{3})^2 + 1)y^2. \\]\n\n   \\[ (2 - \\sqrt{3})^2 = 4 - 4\\sqrt{3} + 3 = 7 - 4\\sqrt{3}. \\]\n\n   \\[ x^2 + y^2 = (8 - 4\\sqrt{3})y^2. \\]\n\nSince \\(x^2 + y^2 = 4xy\\), and we have \\(x = ky\\), we can express \\(xy\\) as:\n\n\\[ xy = ky^2. \\]\n\nThus, \\(4xy = 4ky^2\\), and we have:\n\n\\[ x^2 + y^2 = 4ky^2. \\]\n\nThe minimum value of \\(4ky^2\\) occurs when \\(k\\) is minimized. Since \\(k = 2 \\pm \\sqrt{3}\\), the minimum value of \\(k\\) is \\(2 - \\sqrt{3}\\).\n\nTherefore, the minimum value of \\(x^2 + y^2\\) is:\n\n\\[ 4(2 - \\sqrt{3})y^2. \\]\n\nSince \\(x^2 + y^2 = 4xy\\), and \\(xy = ky^2\\), we have:\n\n\\[ x^2 + y^2 = 4(2 - \\sqrt{3})y^2 = 4xy. \\]\n\nThus, the minimum value of \\(x^2 + y^2\\) is:\n\n\\[ \\boxed{4}. \\]"
'To solve the equation \\( f(f(x)) = x \\) where \\( f(x) = |3x - 1| \\), we first need to understand the behavior of the function \\( f(x) \\).\n\nThe function \\( f(x) = |3x - 1| \\) can be expressed as:\n\\[\nf(x) = \n\\begin{cases} \n3x - 'To solve this problem, we need to find the number of positive integers \\( m \\) such that there exists at least one positive integer \\( n \\) satisfying the inequality \\( m \\cdot n \\le m + n \\).\n\nWe start by rearranging the inequality:\n\n\\[\nm \\cdot n \\le m + n \\implies m \\cdot n - n \\le m \\implies n(m - 1) \\le m.\n\\]\n\nDividing both sides by \\( m - 1 \\) (assuming \\( m \\neq 1 \\)), we get:\n\n\\[\nn \\le \\frac{m}{m - 1}.\n\\]\n\nThe right-hand side simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{m}{m - 1} = 1 + \\frac{1}{m - 1}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\( n \\) is a positive integer, \\( n \\le 1 + \\frac{1}{m - 1} \\) implies that \\( n \\) can be at most 1 or 2, depending on the value of \\( \\frac{1}{m - 1} \\).\n\n- If \\( m = 1 \\), then \\( m \\cdot n = n \\le 1 + n \\) is always true for any positive integer \\( n \\).\n\n- For \\( m > 1 \\), we need \\( n \\le 1 + \\frac{1}{m - 1} \\). Since \\( \\frac{1}{m - 1} < 1 \\) for \\( m > 2 \\), \\( n \\) must be 1.\n\nThus, for \\( m = 1 \\), any \\( n \\) works. 'To solve this problem, we need to find the ordered pair \\((a, b)\\) such that the quadratic \\(x^2 + ax + b\\) has a non-real root whose cube is \\(343\\).\n\nFirst, note that \\(343 = 7^3\\). Therefore, the non-real root of the quadratic must be a complex number whose cube is \\(7\\).\n\nLet the non-real root be \\(z\\). We have \\(z^3 = 7\\). Since \\(z\\) is a non-real root of a quadratic with real coefficients, its complex conjugate \\(\\overline{z}\\) must also be a root of the quadratic.\n\nThe quadratic can be expressed as:\n\\[\n(x - z)(x - \\overline{z}) = x^2 - (z + \\overline{z})x + z\\overline{z}\n\\]\n\nFrom this, we identify:\n- The sum of the roots: \\(z + \\overline{z} = -a\\)\n- The product of the roots: \\(z\\overline{z} = b\\)\n\nSince \\(z\\) and \\(\\overline{z}\\) are complex conjugates, we have:\n\\[\nz = re^{i\\theta}, \\quad \\overline{z} = re^{-i\\theta}\n\\]\nwhere \\(r = |z|\\) and \\(\\theta\\) is the argument of \\(z\\).\n\nThe product \\(z\\overline{z} = r^2\\), so \\(b = r^2\\).\n\nGiven \\(z^3 = 7\\), we have:\n\\[\n(r e^{i\\theta})^3 = 7 \\implies r^3 e^{i3\\theta} = 7\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n- \\(r^3 = 7\\), so \\(r = \\sqrt[3]{7}\\)\n- \\(e^{i3\\theta} = 1\\), which means \\(3\\theta = 2k\\pi\\) for some integer \\(k\\), so \\(\\theta = \\frac{2k\\pi}{3}\\)\n\nThe simplest non-real solution occurs when \\(k = 1\\), giving \\(\\theta = \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThus, the roots are:\n\\[\nz = \\sqrt[3]{7} e^{i\\frac{2\\pi}{3}}, \\quad \\overline{z} = \\sqrt[3]{7} e^{-i\\frac{2\\pi}{3}}\n\\]\n\nThe sum of the roots is:\n\\[\nz + \\overline{z} = \\sqrt[3]{7} \\left( e^{i\\frac{2\\pi}{3}} + e^{-i\\frac{2\\pi}{3}} \\right) = \\sqrt[3]{7} \\cdot 2 \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\right) = \\sqrt[3]{7} \\cdot 2 \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right) = -\\sqrt[3]{7}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(a = \\sqrt[3]{7}\\).\n\nThe product of the roots is:\n\\[\nz\\overline{z} = \\left(\\sqrt[3]{7}\\right)^2 = \\sqrt[3]{49}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(b = \\sqrt[3]{49}\\).\n\nTherefore, the ordered pair \\((a, b)\\) is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\left(\\sqrt[3]{7}, \\sqrt[3]{49}\\right)}\n\\]'
